          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug

          abstract of this manuscript.


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concierto with each other in specifico
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is base on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantl
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating, groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal,
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

        output: ' In a coordinated manner, genes interact with one another within specific contexts to perform their functions. Understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates elucidating the mechanisms of expression regulation across various conditions. This insight is crucial for developing novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped reveal the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease onset and progression. We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally crucial players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, facilitating the identification of disease etiologies and drug mechanisms.'

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concierto with each other in specifico
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is base on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantl
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating, groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal,
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

        output: >2-
           The study of gene expression patterns is crucial in understanding the mechanisms underlying complex traits and the development of new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on individual genes' roles, modern models suggest that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease origin and progression. Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We demonstrate that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies, providing a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.

          1. Context: The study of gene expression patterns is crucial in

          development of new therapies, while transcriptome-wide association
          studies have shed light on individual genes' roles. However, modern
          models suggest that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in
          disease origin and progression.


          2. Content: We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that
          maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data
          into a common latent representation for joint analysis based on
          modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same
          conditions.

          prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion,
          by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can
          contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed
          by single-gene strategies, providing a novel framework for
          interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.

      response:
        output: >2-
           Gene interactions play a pivotal role in the expression of complex traits, and understanding their regulation across different contexts is essential for developing new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on individual gene functions in disease mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions contribute significantly to disease etiology and progression. Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and PhenoPLIER accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infer mechanisms of action. In a CRISPR screen analyzing lipid regulation, we discover that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. This novel framework provides a new lens for interpreting genetic associations through the perspective of gene modules, highlighting disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

          Keywords: Genetic Studies, Functional Genomics, Gene Co-Expression,
          Therapeutic Targets, Drug Repurposing, Clustering of Complex Traits

        output: ' In the field of functional genomics, understanding the influence of genes on complex traits requires a comprehensive examination of gene expression patterns across various conditions. This knowledge is essential for developing novel therapies and unveiling the mechanisms underlying diseases. While transcriptome-wide association studies have revealed the roles of individual genes in disease-related processes, it has become apparent that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease onset and progression. To address this gap in our understanding, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We demonstrate that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lacking associations are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, PhenoPLIER''s ability to contextualize genetic associations through the lens of co-expressed gene modules can reveal potential targets that may be missed by single-gene strategies, offering a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations and advancing our understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.'

           Genes collaborate in specific contexts to perform their functions, and understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates a mechanistic comprehension of expression regulation across various conditions. This insight is crucial for developing innovative therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped elucidate the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms; however, modern models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease origin and progression. In this study, we present PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, facilitating disease etiology and drug mechanism understanding in functional genomics studies.

          Keywords: genetic studies, functional genomics, gene co-expression,
          therapeutic targets, drug repurposing, clustering of complex traits,
          PhenoPLIER, gene expression patterns, latent representation, CRISPR
          screen, lipid regulation, disease etiology, drug mechanisms.

          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on

          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          act in concert with each other in specific contexts to perform their

          requires a mechanistic understanding of expression regulation across
          different conditions. It has been shown that this insight is critical

          have helped uncover the role of individual genes in disease-relevant
          mechanisms. However, modern models of the architecture of complex
          traits predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in
          disease origin and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based
          on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same
          conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly associated with
          gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is
          accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring
          mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze
          lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
          associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context

          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
          associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
          functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach
          that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation
          data into a common latent representation for a joint analysis. Genes
          act in concert with each other in specific contexts to perform their
          functions. Determining how these genes influence complex traits
          requires a mechanistic understanding of expression regulation across
          different conditions. It has been shown that this insight is critical
          for developing new therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies
          have helped uncover the role of individual genes in disease-relevant
          mechanisms. However, modern models of the architecture of complex
          traits predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in
          disease origin and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based
          on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same
          conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly associated with
          gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is
          accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring
          mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze
          lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
          associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed
          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

However, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease origin and progression, making it essential to study the expression regulation of genes across different conditions. Existing methods, such as transcriptome-wide association studies, have focused on the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. To address this gap, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. Our method is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, which are significantly associated with diseases in relevant cell types. PhenoPLIER''s accuracy in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of action demonstrates its potential as a powerful tool for interpreting genetic associations through gene modules. By incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. This novel framework provides a new perspective on understanding disease etiology and drug mechanisms by highlighting the importance of gene expression patterns in complex traits.'

          genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal
          potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER
          provides a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations
          through the lens of gene modules.

          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").

Our results demonstrate that PhenoPLIER can provide a more comprehensive view of genetic associations, revealing potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. Overall, PhenoPLIER offers a powerful tool for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, shedding light on disease etiology and drug mechanisms while also highlighting potential therapeutic targets that were previously overlooked.'

Understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates a mechanistic understanding of expression regulation across various conditions. This insight is critical for developing new therapies; however, there is currently a gap in the literature regarding the accurate prediction and analysis of known drug-disease pairs and the inference of mechanisms of action. Our study aimed to address this gap by utilizing PhenoPLIER, an approach that associates diseases with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types. Through our investigation, we discovered that PhenoPLIER can accurately predict known drug-disease pairs and infer mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we found that functionally significant players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, thereby contributing to the broader field of functional genomics and therapeutic target identification.'

          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in

          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concert with each other in specific
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER.

While transcriptome-wide association studies have provided valuable insights into individual gene roles, the modern architecture of complex traits suggests that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease progression. To address this gap, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is built on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across conditions, allowing us to identify diseases significantly associated with gene modules in relevant cell types. PhenoPLIER demonstrates accuracy in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen for lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. These findings highlight the potential of PhenoPLIER in advancing our understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms, paving the way for more effective therapeutic targets and drug repurposing strategies.'

           The study of genes in complex traits requires a thorough understanding of their expression regulation across different conditions, as this knowledge is essential for the development of new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have provided insights into the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression. In this context, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. Based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Additionally, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. This approach fills the gap in current literature by focusing on gene-gene interactions and demonstrates its potential in enhancing our understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

          traits by developing PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for joint analysis. Our method is based
          on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same
          conditions, which allows us to accurately predict known drug-disease
          pairs and infer mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen
          to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important
          players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated
          modules by PhenoPLIER. This novel approach not only contributes to the
          existing literature on gene-gene interactions but also provides
          valuable insights into disease etiology and drug mechanisms,
          highlighting its broader significance in the field of functional
          genomics and therapeutic targets.

          mapping gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data
          into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This method
          accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of
          action, as demonstrated by its ability to prioritize functionally
          important players in trait-associated modules using a CRISPR screen
          for lipid regulation. Our work not only enhances the understanding of
          disease etiology and drug mechanisms but also has broader implications
          for the fields of genetic studies, functional genomics, gene
          co-expression, therapeutic targets, and drug repurposing.

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
          improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic

          etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,

          abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure and
          follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits in a
          single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary
          to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
          follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to the
          reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients the
          reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
          research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands on
          the open question that the research answered. A successful context
          section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s contributions
          from the current state of the art by communicating what is missing in
          the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e.,
          the connection between the specific gap and the broader context that
          the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the
          novel method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question.
          Then you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
          Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the question
          that was posed at the end of the context section. There is often a
          second part to the conclusion section that highlights how this
          conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e., "broader
          significance").


          Input paragraph: Genes act in concert with each other in specific
          contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
          influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
          expression regulation across different conditions. It has been shown
          that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
          Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of
          individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However, modern
          models of the architecture of complex traits predict that gene-gene
          interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression.
          Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps
          gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a
          common latent representation for a joint analysis. This representation
          is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across
          the same conditions. We observe that diseases are significantly
          associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our
          approach is accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and
          inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to
          analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players
          lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
          PhenoPLIER.

While transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression. Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. Our results show that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. This approach contributes to the field by providing a new method for understanding gene-gene interactions and their role in disease etiology and drug mechanisms.'

           Genes collaborate to perform their functions in specific contexts, necessitating a comprehensive understanding of expression regulation across various conditions for deciphering how they influence complex traits. This insight is pivotal in devising novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have elucidated the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions significantly contribute to disease onset and progression. We present PhenoPLIER, a computational method that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. In a CRISPR screen examining lipid regulation, we find that functionally crucial components lack associations but are prioritized within trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER.

            paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely necessary to
            follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the abstract must
            follow the following structure: 1) The context must communicate to
            the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first sentence orients
            the reader by introducing the broader field in which the particular
            research is situated. Then, this context is narrowed until it lands
            on the open question that the research answered. A successful
            context section sets the stage for distinguishing the paper’s
            contributions from the current state of the art by communicating
            what is missing in the literature (i.e., the specific gap) and why
            that matters (i.e., the connection between the specific gap and the
            broader context that the paper opened with); 2) The content ("Here
            we") first describes the novel method or approach that you used to
            fill the gap or question. Then you present the meat—your executive
            summary of the results; 3) Finally, the conclusion interprets the
            results to answer the question that was posed at the end of the
            context section. There is often a second part to the conclusion
            section that highlights how this conclusion moves the broader field
            forward (i.e., "broader significance").

            text: ' In a coordinated manner, genes interact with one another within specific contexts to perform their functions. Understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates elucidating the mechanisms of expression regulation across various conditions. This insight is crucial for developing novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped reveal the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease onset and progression. We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally crucial players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, facilitating the identification of disease etiologies and drug mechanisms.'

              ---
               Gene interactions play a pivotal role in the expression of complex traits, and understanding their regulation across different contexts is essential for developing new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on individual gene functions in disease mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions contribute significantly to disease etiology and progression. Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and PhenoPLIER accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infer mechanisms of action. In a CRISPR screen analyzing lipid regulation, we discover that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. This novel framework provides a new lens for interpreting genetic associations through the perspective of gene modules, highlighting disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

              ---
               Genes collaborate in specific contexts to perform their functions, and understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates a mechanistic comprehension of expression regulation across various conditions. This insight is crucial for developing innovative therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped elucidate the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms; however, modern models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease origin and progression. In this study, we present PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, facilitating disease etiology and drug mechanism understanding in functional genomics studies.

            text: ' In the context of complex traits, understanding how genes interact with each other in specific contexts is crucial for developing new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a key role in disease origin and progression.             text: ' Genes function in concert with one another, interacting in specific contexts to perform their roles. Understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates a comprehensive grasp of expression regulation across various conditions. This understanding is crucial for the development of novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped elucidate the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease initiation and progression. In this study, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally significant players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, incorporating groups of co-expressed genes enables PhenoPLIER to contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets that may be missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, providing valuable insights into disease etiology and drug mechanisms.'

              ---
               Genes interact in specific contexts to perform their functions, and understanding how these interactions influence complex traits requires a comprehensive analysis of gene expression regulation across different conditions. This insight is crucial for the development of novel therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on the role of individual genes in disease mechanisms, modern models suggest that gene-gene interactions play a pivotal role in disease onset and progression. Here, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Moreover, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we discover that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.

              ---
               We present PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for comprehensive analysis. Understanding the collaborative role of genes in specific contexts is essential for unveiling the mechanisms behind complex traits and developing novel therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have elucidated the individual roles of genes in disease-related processes, modern architectural models predict that gene-gene interactions significantly contribute to disease onset and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based on groups of genes with similar expression patterns across various conditions. Our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action by associating diseases with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types. Additionally, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally significant players are not associated with single genes but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, PhenoPLIER's incorporation of co-expressed gene groups contextualizes genetic associations and identifies potential targets overlooked by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for decoding genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, ultimately enhancing our understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

               This study aims to address the gap in understanding the role of gene-gene interactions in disease origin and progression by introducing PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. The context is set by highlighting the importance of transcriptome-wide association studies in revealing individual genes' roles in disease-relevant mechanisms but noting that modern models predict gene-gene interactions play a critical role in disease etiology and progression. In conclusion, PhenoPLIER's incorporation of co-expressed gene groups provides a framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, revealing potential targets missed by single-gene strategies and contributing to the understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

              ---
               We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that synthesizes gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a unified latent representation for joint analysis. Recognizing the concerted action of genes in specific contexts is crucial for elucidating their roles in complex traits and informing novel therapeutic strategies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on the contributions of individual genes to disease mechanisms, but modern models highlight the importance of gene-gene interactions in disease etiology and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based on modules of co-expressed genes across different conditions. We demonstrate that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, PhenoPLIER's integration of co-expressed gene groups enables contextualization of genetic associations and reveals potential targets missed by single-gene approaches. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, with applications in disease etiology and drug mechanism elucidation.

              ---
               We present PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that synthesizes gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a unified latent representation for joint analysis. Genes function collaboratively in specific contexts to execute their roles. Gaining an understanding of how these genes influence complex traits necessitates insight into expression regulation across diverse conditions, which is crucial for the development of novel therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have helped elucidate individual gene contributions to disease mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions are essential in determining disease onset and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is constructed using modules of co-expressed genes across the same conditions. Our analysis demonstrates significant associations between diseases and gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, as well as accurate predictions of known drug-disease pairs and inferred mechanisms of action. Using a CRISPR screen to study lipid regulation, we find that PhenoPLIER prioritizes functionally important players that lack associations but are situated in trait-associated modules. In summary, by integrating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations and unveils potential targets missed by single-gene approaches. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, with potential applications in disease etiology and drug mechanism elucidation.

              ---
               In the field of genetic studies, understanding gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data is crucial for developing new therapies. However, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease origin and progression, making it essential to study the expression regulation of genes across different conditions. Existing methods, such as transcriptome-wide association studies, have focused on the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. To address this gap, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. Our method is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, which are significantly associated with diseases in relevant cell types. PhenoPLIER's accuracy in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of action demonstrates its potential as a powerful tool for interpreting genetic associations through gene modules. By incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene strategies. This novel framework provides a new perspective on understanding disease etiology and drug mechanisms by highlighting the importance of gene expression patterns in complex traits.

               We present PhenoPLIER, a computational method that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a unified latent representation for comprehensive analysis. Genes collaborate to perform their functions in specific contexts; understanding how they influence complex traits necessitates insight into expression regulation across various conditions. This knowledge is crucial for discovering novel therapies. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations and reveals potential targets overlooked by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, facilitating the identification of functional relationships between genes and diseases.

               The field of genetic studies has made significant progress in understanding the role of individual genes in disease mechanisms; however, a comprehensive view of gene-gene interactions is crucial for elucidating the origins and progression of complex traits. In this study, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis.               ---
               We introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for comprehensive analysis. Genes collaborate in specific contexts to perform their functions; understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates insight into expression regulation across different conditions, crucial for the development of novel therapies. Traditional transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on individual gene roles in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of complex trait architecture suggest that gene-gene interactions are critical in disease origin and progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We find that diseases are significantly correlated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. Additionally, through a CRISPR screen analyzing lipid regulation, we discover that functionally significant players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating co-expressed gene groups, PhenoPLIER contextualizes genetic associations and identifies potential targets overlooked by single-gene strategies. PhenoPLIER offers a novel framework for interpreting genetic associations through the lens of gene modules, thereby highlighting disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

              ---
               The study of genes in complex traits requires a thorough understanding of their expression regulation across different conditions, as this knowledge is essential for the development of new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have provided insights into the role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms, modern models predict that gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and progression. In this context, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. Based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions, our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Additionally, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. This approach fills the gap in current literature by focusing on gene-gene interactions and demonstrates its potential in enhancing our understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.

              In this study, we address a critical gap in the field of complex
              traits by developing PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that
              maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data
              into a common latent representation for joint analysis. Our method
              is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns
              across the same conditions, which allows us to accurately predict
              known drug-disease pairs and infer mechanisms of action. Moreover,
              using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that
              functionally important players lack associations but are
              prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. This novel
              approach not only contributes to the existing literature on
              gene-gene interactions but also provides valuable insights into
              disease etiology and drug mechanisms, highlighting its broader
              significance in the field of functional genomics and therapeutic
              targets.


              In summary, our study introduces PhenoPLIER, a computational
              approach that fills the gap in the literature on gene-gene
              interactions by mapping gene-trait associations and
              pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent
              representation for joint analysis. This method accurately predicts
              known drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action, as
              demonstrated by its ability to prioritize functionally important
              players in trait-associated modules using a CRISPR screen for
              lipid regulation. Our work not only enhances the understanding of
              disease etiology and drug mechanisms but also has broader
              implications for the fields of genetic studies, functional
              genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, and drug
              repurposing.

              abstract must follow the following structure: 1) The context must
              communicate to the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first
              sentence orients the reader by introducing the broader field in
              which the particular research is situated. Then, this context is
              narrowed until it lands on the open question that the research
              answered. A successful context section sets the stage for
              distinguishing the paper’s contributions from the current state of
              the art by communicating what is missing in the literature (i.e.,
              the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e., the connection
              between the specific gap and the broader context that the paper
              opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the novel
              method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question. Then
              you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
              Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the
              question that was posed at the end of the context section. There
              is often a second part to the conclusion section that highlights
              how this conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e.,
              "broader significance").

               Genes collaborate to perform their functions in specific contexts, and understanding how these genes influence complex traits necessitates a comprehensive grasp of expression regulation across various conditions. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits propose that gene-gene interactions are pivotal to disease onset and progression.               ---
               Genes collaborate to perform their functions in specific contexts, necessitating a comprehensive understanding of expression regulation across various conditions for deciphering how they influence complex traits. This insight is pivotal in devising novel therapies. Transcriptome-wide association studies have elucidated the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms. However, contemporary models of the architecture of complex traits suggest that gene-gene interactions significantly contribute to disease onset and progression. We present PhenoPLIER, a computational method that integrates gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a shared latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on groups of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. Our approach demonstrates significant association between diseases and gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and it accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and elucidates mechanisms of action. In a CRISPR screen examining lipid regulation, we find that functionally crucial components lack associations but are prioritized within trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER.

            text: ' Understanding the interplay of genes in specific contexts is essential for determining how they influence complex traits and informing the development of new therapies. While transcriptome-wide association studies have elucidated the role of individual genes in disease-related mechanisms, modern models suggest that gene-gene interactions play a significant role in disease origin and progression. To address this gap, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a common latent representation for joint analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions. Our results show that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types, and PhenoPLIER accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. Overall, our approach provides new insights into the genetic architecture of complex traits and contributes to the understanding of disease etiology and drug mechanisms.'

              You are a scientist with copy-editing skills who will help in
              improving the text of a manuscript with title 'Projecting genetic
              associations through gene expression patterns highlights disease
              etiology and drug mechanisms' and keywords 'genetic studies,
              functional genomics, gene co-expression, therapeutic targets, drug
              repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Revise the following
              abstract of this manuscript, so it has a clear sentence structure
              and follows a context-content-conclusion (C-C-C) scheme and fits
              in a single paragraph. Make changes only when they are absolutely
              necessary to follow the C-C-C scheme. Under the C-C-C scheme, the
              abstract must follow the following structure: 1) The context must
              communicate to the reader what gap the paper will fill. The first
              sentence orients the reader by introducing the broader field in
              which the particular research is situated. Then, this context is
              narrowed until it lands on the open question that the research
              answered. A successful context section sets the stage for
              distinguishing the paper’s contributions from the current state of
              the art by communicating what is missing in the literature (i.e.,
              the specific gap) and why that matters (i.e., the connection
              between the specific gap and the broader context that the paper
              opened with); 2) The content ("Here we") first describes the novel
              method or approach that you used to fill the gap or question. Then
              you present the meat—your executive summary of the results; 3)
              Finally, the conclusion interprets the results to answer the
              question that was posed at the end of the context section. There
              is often a second part to the conclusion section that highlights
              how this conclusion moves the broader field forward (i.e.,
              "broader significance").


              Input paragraph: Genes act in concert with each other in specific
              contexts to perform their functions. Determining how these genes
              influence complex traits requires a mechanistic understanding of
              expression regulation across different conditions. It has been
              shown that this insight is critical for developing new therapies.
              Transcriptome-wide association studies have helped uncover the
              role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However,
              modern models of the architecture of complex traits predict that
              gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and
              progression. Here we introduce PhenoPLIER, a computational
              approach that maps gene-trait associations and pharmacological
              perturbation data into a common latent representation for a joint
              analysis. This representation is based on modules of genes with
              similar expression patterns across the same conditions. We observe
              that diseases are significantly associated with gene modules
              expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is accurate in
              predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring mechanisms of
              action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to analyze lipid
              regulation, we find that functionally important players lack
              associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules by
              PhenoPLIER.
